1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Rainhill_Bridge%2C_from_Bury%27s_Liverpool_and_Manchester_Railway%2C_1831_-_artfinder_3408.jpg/400px-Rainhill_Bridge%2C_from_Bury%27s_Liverpool_and_Manchester_Railway%2C_1831_-_artfinder_3408.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="817" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rainhill_Bridge,_from_Bury%27s_Liverpool_and_Manchester_Railway,_1831_-_artfinder_3408.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rainhill_Skew_Bridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Rainhill Skew Bridge">Rainhill Skew Bridge</a> in 1831</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_and_Manchester_Railway" title="Liverpool and Manchester Railway">Liverpool and Manchester Railway</a> was the world\'s first passenger <a href="/wiki/Inter-city_rail" title="Inter-city rail">inter-city railway</a> in 1830. <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Liverpool_Road_railway_station" title="Manchester Liverpool Road railway station">Manchester Liverpool Road railway station</a> is the world\'s oldest surviving railway station, having opened on 15 September 1830; the <a href="/wiki/Stockton_and_Darlington_Railway" title="Stockton and Darlington Railway">Stockton and Darlington Railway</a> had opened in 1825. <a href="/wiki/Chat_Moss" title="Chat Moss">Chat Moss</a> was a problem to constructing the railway, with <a href="/wiki/Wapping_Tunnel" title="Wapping Tunnel">Edge Hill Tunnel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sankey_Viaduct" title="Sankey Viaduct">Sankey Viaduct</a>; the line was bitterly opposed by <a href="/wiki/William_Molyneux,_2nd_Earl_of_Sefton" title="William Molyneux, 2nd Earl of Sefton">William Molyneux, 2nd Earl of Sefton</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bridgewater_Canal" title="Bridgewater Canal">Bridgewater Canal</a> was the first recognised canal of the modern era. <a href="/wiki/Francis_Egerton,_3rd_Duke_of_Bridgewater" title="Francis Egerton, 3rd Duke of Bridgewater">Francis Egerton, 3rd Duke of Bridgewater</a> had visited France and noted their canals. <a href="/wiki/John_Gilbert_(agent)" title="John Gilbert (agent)">John Gilbert</a> had the innovative idea to use water pumped out of his coal mines to fill a canal from the Duke\'s Worsley mines to Manchester. It was designed by <a href="/wiki/James_Brindley" title="James Brindley">James Brindley</a> and built in 1761.\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacture_during_the_Industrial_Revolution" title="Textile manufacture during the Industrial Revolution">Textile manufacture during the Industrial Revolution</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nasmyth,_Gaskell_and_Company" title="Nasmyth, Gaskell and Company">Bridgewater Foundry</a> in <a href="/wiki/Patricroft" title="Patricroft">Patricroft</a> (Salford), can claim to be the world\'s first factory\nwith an <a href="/wiki/Assembly_line" title="Assembly line">assembly line</a> type arrangement in 1836. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Huddart" title="Joseph Huddart">Joseph Huddart</a> of Cumbria was the first to mechanise the production of rope in 1793. The <a href="/wiki/Spinning_jenny" title="Spinning jenny">spinning jenny</a> was invented in 1764 in Lancashire by <a href="/wiki/James_Hargreaves" title="James Hargreaves">James Hargreaves</a>, a mechanical advance on the <a href="/wiki/Spinning_wheel" title="Spinning wheel">spinning wheel</a>.\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_computing_hardware" title="History of computing hardware">History of computing hardware</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Manchester" title="University of Manchester">University of Manchester</a> built the world\'s first <a href="/wiki/Stored-program_computer" title="Stored-program computer">programmable computer</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Baby" title="Manchester Baby">Manchester Baby</a>, on 21 June 1948; the <a href="/wiki/Williams_tube" title="Williams tube">Williams\xe2\x80\x93Kilburn tube</a> on the machine was the world\'s first computer memory, and the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Random-access_memory" title="Random-access memory">random-access memory</a> (RAM); the baby computer was made from 550 <a href="/wiki/Mullard" title="Mullard">Mullard</a> valves. The first commercially available computer, the <a href="/wiki/Ferranti_Mark_1" title="Ferranti Mark 1">Ferranti Mark 1</a>, was made in Manchester and sold in February 1951 to the University of Manchester. The world\'s first <a href="/wiki/Transistor_computer" title="Transistor computer">transitorised computer</a> was the Manchester Transistor Computer in November 1953. <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(computer)" title="Atlas (computer)">Atlas</a> was another important computer developed at the University of Manchester, largely developed by <a href="/wiki/Tom_Kilburn" title="Tom Kilburn">Tom Kilburn</a>; at the time in 1962 it was most powerful computer in the world. The government had dropped its financial support of this computer, and was only funded by Ferranti\xe2\x80\x94the total development cost was around \xc2\xa31m. Britain was leading the world at this time in computing, with the only main competitor being IBM; after the mid-1950s America took over the industry. The spreadsheet was invented in 1974, known as the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Works_Record_System&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Works Record System (page does not exist)">Works Record System</a>, and used an <a href="/wiki/ADABAS" title="ADABAS">ADABAS</a> database on a <a href="/wiki/IBM_3270" title="IBM 3270">IBM 3270</a> at ICI in Northwich; it was developed by Dr <a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Mais&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Robert Mais (page does not exist)">Robert Mais</a> and it was around four years before (the more well-known) <a href="/wiki/VisiCalc" title="VisiCalc">VisiCalc</a> in 1978. The University of Manchester has collected 25 Nobel prizes, though recent years have been less notable.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Parsonage_Colliery" title="Parsonage Colliery">Parsonage Colliery</a> at Leigh had the UK\'s deepest mine\xe2\x80\x941,260 metres in 1949. Macclesfield was the base of UK\'s <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> weaving industry. <a href="/wiki/John_Benjamin_Dancer" title="John Benjamin Dancer">John Benjamin Dancer</a> of Manchester invented <a href="/wiki/Microphotograph" title="Microphotograph">microphotography</a> in 1839, which would lead to <a href="/wiki/Microform" title="Microform">microform</a> in the 1920s. <a href="/wiki/Frank_Hornby" title="Frank Hornby">Frank Hornby</a> from Liverpool invented <a href="/wiki/Meccano" title="Meccano">Meccano</a> in 1901, where <a href="/wiki/Meccano_Ltd" title="Meccano Ltd">Meccano Ltd</a> would be based for over 60 years. <a href="/wiki/Bryant_and_May" title="Bryant and May">Bryant and May</a>\'s site in <a href="/wiki/Garston,_Merseyside" class="mw-redirect" title="Garston, Merseyside">Garston</a> was the last wooden <a href="/wiki/Match" title="Match">match</a> factory in the UK, closing in 1994 to become <a href="/wiki/Mersey_Match_Factory" title="Mersey Match Factory">The Matchworks</a> business centre off the A561 west of the former Speke airport. <a href="/wiki/Cottonopolis" title="Cottonopolis">Cottonopolis</a> was the industrial name for Manchester and the local area. Manchester at one time was the world\'s richest city. The <a href="/wiki/CIS_Tower" title="CIS Tower">CIS Tower</a>, built by <a href="/wiki/Laing_O%27Rourke" title="Laing O&#39;Rourke">John Laing</a> in 1962, was Europe\'s tallest building, and Britain\'s tallest building until 1963, and Manchester\'s tallest building until 2006.\n</p><p>Kirkby was planned in the 1950s as the largest <a href="/wiki/Industrial_park" title="Industrial park">trading estate</a> in Britain\xe2\x80\x941,800 acres. Trafford Park is the world\'s first planned industrial estate. <a href="/wiki/Rochdale_Society_of_Equitable_Pioneers" title="Rochdale Society of Equitable Pioneers">Rochdale Society of Equitable Pioneers</a> opened their first co-operative outlet on 21 December 1844.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_World_of_Glass_Museum,_St_Helens_-_geograph.org.uk_-_280250.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/The_World_of_Glass_Museum%2C_St_Helens_-_geograph.org.uk_-_280250.jpg/220px-The_World_of_Glass_Museum%2C_St_Helens_-_geograph.org.uk_-_280250.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/The_World_of_Glass_Museum%2C_St_Helens_-_geograph.org.uk_-_280250.jpg/330px-The_World_of_Glass_Museum%2C_St_Helens_-_geograph.org.uk_-_280250.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/The_World_of_Glass_Museum%2C_St_Helens_-_geograph.org.uk_-_280250.jpg/440px-The_World_of_Glass_Museum%2C_St_Helens_-_geograph.org.uk_-_280250.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_World_of_Glass_Museum,_St_Helens_-_geograph.org.uk_-_280250.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/w/index.php?title=World_of_Glass_(St_Helens)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="World of Glass (St Helens) (page does not exist)">World of Glass</a> museum in October 2006</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Alastair_Pilkington" title="Alastair Pilkington">Alastair Pilkington</a> invented the <a href="/wiki/Float_glass" title="Float glass">float glass</a> method of manufacture in 1957 at St Helens, announcing it in January 1959. It was manufacturered from 1961, and 80% of the world\'s glass is made with the process; the former site closed in 2014 and it is made now at the Green Gate site. <a href="/wiki/Pears_soap" class="mw-redirect" title="Pears soap">Pears soap</a>, made at Port Sunlight, is the world\'s first registered brand, and world\'s oldest brand in existence. <a href="/wiki/Elihu_Thomson" title="Elihu Thomson">Elihu Thomson</a>, born in Manchester who subsequently moved to America, formed <a href="/wiki/Thomson-CSF" title="Thomson-CSF">Thomson-CSF</a> which became <a href="/wiki/Thales_Group" title="Thales Group">Thales Group</a> in 2000. The British part (<a href="/wiki/British_Thomson-Houston" title="British Thomson-Houston">British Thomson-Houston</a>) would later become part of GEC; he invented the <a href="/wiki/Arc_lamp" title="Arc lamp">arc lamp</a>. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Brunner" title="Henry Brunner">Henry Brunner</a> from Liverpool would join with <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Mond" title="Ludwig Mond">Ludwig Mond</a> in the 1860s to form a chemical company which became ICI in 1926. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mossbay_Steelworks&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mossbay Steelworks (page does not exist)">Mossbay Steelworks</a> in Workington, when opened in 1877, were the world\'s first large-scale steelworks; its <a href="/wiki/Austenite" title="Austenite">austenitic</a> manganese steel (<a href="/wiki/Mangalloy" title="Mangalloy">mangalloy</a>) was produced from 1877 until 1974, with Britain\'s railways converting from iron to steel by the 1880s. Track was made there for the UK\'s railways (exclusively from the 1970s onwards, with the steel made in Teesside) until August 2006; much of the rails made were exported (from 1882), with its main competitor being <a href="/wiki/Voestalpine" title="Voestalpine">Voestalpine</a> of Austria, and a plant (bought by British Steel in 1999) in <a href="/wiki/Hayange" title="Hayange">Hayange</a>, France, who make all of <a href="/wiki/SNCF" title="SNCF">SNCF</a>\'s railway tracks, and the <a href="/wiki/Katowice_Steelworks" title="Katowice Steelworks">Katowice Steelworks</a> in Poland. Workington was thought to make the best quality <a href="/wiki/Track_(rail_transport)" title="Track (rail transport)">rail track</a> in the world.\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/War_of_Currents" class="mw-redirect" title="War of Currents">War of Currents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mains_electricity" title="Mains electricity">Mains electricity</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Sebastian_Ziani_de_Ferranti" title="Sebastian Ziani de Ferranti">Sebastian Ziani de Ferranti</a>, born in Liverpool in 1864, was an <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineering" title="Electrical engineering">electrical engineer</a> who designed the layout for <a href="/wiki/Deptford_Power_Station" title="Deptford Power Station">Deptford Power Station</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/AC_power" title="AC power">alternating current</a> <a href="/wiki/Power_station" title="Power station">power station</a> in the world in 1887, and whose design all others would follow; his later company <a href="/wiki/Ferranti" title="Ferranti">Ferranti</a>, of Oldham, would later be an industry leader in Britain\'s defence electronics, on the <a href="/wiki/FTSE_100_Index" title="FTSE 100 Index">FTSE 100 Index</a>. Ferranti\'s design of increasing AC voltage to <a href="/wiki/High_voltage" title="High voltage">high tension</a> at the power station, to be stepped-down at a transformer at <a href="/wiki/Electrical_substation" title="Electrical substation">substations</a> before entering properties, is the system all electricity networks take today; the system reduces wasteful heating of electricity transmission cables.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_(11823864155).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg/200px-Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg" width="200" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg/300px-Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg/400px-Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3200" data-file-height="2558" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_(11823864155).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Calder Hall in 1973</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Chain_Home" title="Chain Home">Chain Home</a> radar transmitters were built by Metrovick at its Trafford Park Works, which became part of <a href="/wiki/Associated_Electrical_Industries" title="Associated Electrical Industries">AEI</a> in 1929, <a href="/wiki/General_Electric_Company_plc" class="mw-redirect" title="General Electric Company plc">GEC</a> in 1968, and as <a href="/wiki/Alstom" title="Alstom">Alstom</a> it was closed in June 2000. <a href="/wiki/2ZY" title="2ZY">2ZY</a>, the first broadcasts in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">north of England</a>, were made from the Metrovick factory in November 1922, which became part of the <a href="/wiki/BBC_National_Programme" title="BBC National Programme">BBC National Programme</a> in 1927. GEC opened its first factory in Manchester in 1888, moving to Salford in 1895 at the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peel_Works&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Peel Works (page does not exist)">Peel Works</a>, and had built the <a href="/wiki/Osram" title="Osram">Osram</a> electric light company in 1893. The <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan-Vickers_F.2" title="Metropolitan-Vickers F.2">Metropolitan-Vickers F.2</a> of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Trafford_Park_Works&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Trafford Park Works (page does not exist)">Trafford Park Works</a>, Manchester was the first <a href="/wiki/Axial_compressor" title="Axial compressor">axial-flow</a> jet engine, with a nine-stage compressor, first running in 1941. It would end up as the <a href="/wiki/Armstrong_Siddeley_Sapphire" title="Armstrong Siddeley Sapphire">Armstrong Siddeley Sapphire</a> and the American-built <a href="/wiki/Wright_J65" title="Wright J65">Wright J65</a>. The F.2 gas turbine would power <a href="/w/index.php?title=MGB.2009&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="MGB.2009 (page does not exist)">MGB.2009</a> the first gas-turbine-powered vessel in 1947. <a href="/wiki/No._1_Parachute_Training_School_RAF" title="No. 1 Parachute Training School RAF">No. 1 Parachute Training School RAF</a>\xe2\x80\x94the main parachute training site for the war\xe2\x80\x94was at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Ringway" title="RAF Ringway">RAF Ringway</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Central_Landing_Establishment" title="Central Landing Establishment">Central Landing Establishment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Airborne_Forces_Experimental_Establishment" title="Airborne Forces Experimental Establishment">Airborne Forces Experimental Establishment</a>) now Manchester Airport; many aircraft were built there too, and the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Trafford_Park_Factory" title="Ford Trafford Park Factory">Ford Trafford Park Factory</a> built 34,000 aircraft engines\xe2\x80\x94mostly <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Merlin" title="Rolls-Royce Merlin">Merlin</a> <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_engine" title="Aircraft engine">engines</a>; the nearby Metropolitan-Vickers factory built many Lancasters.\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_by_country" title="Nuclear power by country">Nuclear power by country</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Nuclear power in the United Kingdom">Nuclear power in the United Kingdom</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom" title="Nuclear weapons and the United Kingdom">Nuclear weapons and the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Sellafield" title="Sellafield">Calder Hall</a> was the world\'s first nuclear power station in 1956. There are approximately 430 <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> stations around the world, and the UK is the third most experienced operator of nuclear reactors after the USA and France, and is the world\'s ninth largest producer of nuclear-generated electricity, with <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Nuclear power in the United Kingdom">nine stations operating in the UK</a> producing around 10GW. New-build nuclear power stations will either be the <a href="/wiki/AP1000" title="AP1000">AP1000</a> (Toshiba Westinghouse <a href="/wiki/NuGeneration" title="NuGeneration">NuGeneration</a>) or <a href="/wiki/EPR_(nuclear_reactor)" title="EPR (nuclear reactor)">EPR</a> design (developed by <a href="/wiki/Areva" class="mw-redirect" title="Areva">Areva</a>). BNFL bought Pittsburgh-based <a href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Electric_Company" title="Westinghouse Electric Company">Westinghouse Electric Company</a> in 1999; it was sold in October 2006 for \xc2\xa35.4bn to Toshiba. <a href="/wiki/British_Energy" title="British Energy">British Energy</a> was sold in 2009 for \xc2\xa312.5bn to <a href="/wiki/%C3%89lectricit%C3%A9_de_France" title="\xc3\x89lectricit\xc3\xa9 de France">EDF</a>; <a href="/wiki/Centrica" title="Centrica">Centrica</a> (<a href="/wiki/British_Gas" title="British Gas">British Gas</a>) had also wanted to buy it; 26 Magnox reactors were built in the UK, followed by 14 AGR reactors.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Operation_Hurricane" title="Operation Hurricane">Operation Hurricane</a> on 3 October 1952, Britain\'s first <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear bomb</a>, detonated on <a href="/wiki/HMS_Plym_(K271)" title="HMS Plym (K271)">HMS <i>Plym</i></a> on the <a href="/wiki/Montebello_Islands" title="Montebello Islands">Montebello Islands</a> in the state of <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>, was made of <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">plutonium-239</a> mostly made at Windscale (which began production in 1950), with some possibly from <a href="/wiki/Chalk_River_Laboratories" title="Chalk River Laboratories">Chalk River Laboratories</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, Canada (where the <a href="/wiki/Tube_Alloys" title="Tube Alloys">Tube Alloys</a> project was later moved).\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Siddeley_Nimrod_MR2_(801),_UK_-_Air_Force_AN1914655.jpg" 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Hawker_Siddeley_Nimrod_MR2_%28801%29%2C_UK_-_Air_Force_AN1914655.jpg/400px-Hawker_Siddeley_Nimrod_MR2_%28801%29%2C_UK_-_Air_Force_AN1914655.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Siddeley_Nimrod_MR2_(801),_UK_-_Air_Force_AN1914655.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Siddeley_Nimrod" title="Hawker Siddeley Nimrod">Hawker Siddeley Nimrod</a> MR2 (HS 801), built at Woodford (former Avro) and designed in Manchester in the mid-1960s, with <i>XV148</i> (former Comet 4C) making its <a href="/wiki/John_Cunningham_(RAF_officer)" title="John Cunningham (RAF officer)">first flight</a> on 23 May 1967, flying from Chester (<a href="/wiki/Hawarden_Airport" title="Hawarden Airport">Broughton</a>, which had built many <a href="/wiki/De_Havilland" title="De Havilland">de Havilland</a> fighter jet aircraft) to Woodford; 49 Nimrods were made for the RAF, entering service with <a href="/wiki/No._201_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 201 Squadron RAF">201 Sqn</a> on 6 November 1970, serving until March 2010 with <a href="/wiki/No._38_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 38 Squadron RAF">38 Sqn</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/w/index.php?title=W._T._Glover_%26_Co.&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="W. T. Glover &amp; Co. (page does not exist)">W. T. Glover &amp; Co.</a> of Salford were important electricity cable manufacturers throughout the early 20th century. BAE Systems <a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_Tunnel_Department&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Wind Tunnel Department (page does not exist)">Wind Tunnel Department</a> at Warton\xe2\x80\x94one its four wind tunnels\xe2\x80\x94the High Speed Wind Tunnel\xe2\x80\x94can test speeds intermittently up to Mach 3.8 (<a href="/wiki/Supersonic_speed" title="Supersonic speed">trisonic</a>)\xe2\x80\x94the second fastest in the UK, to the University of Manchester\'s <a href="/w/index.php?title=Aero-Physics_Laboratory&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Aero-Physics Laboratory (page does not exist)">Aero-Physics Laboratory</a> which has a hypersonic wind tunnel up to Mach 6. Prof <a href="/wiki/Osborne_Reynolds" title="Osborne Reynolds">Osborne Reynolds</a> of Owens College (which became the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_University_of_Manchester" title="Victoria University of Manchester">Victoria University of Manchester</a> in 1904), known worldwide for his <a href="/wiki/Reynolds_number" title="Reynolds number">Reynolds number</a> (introduced elsewhere by the mathematician <a href="/wiki/Sir_George_Stokes,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir George Stokes, 1st Baronet">George Gabriel Stokes</a>), showed in the early 1880s that wind tunnels (invented by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Herbert_Wenham" title="Francis Herbert Wenham">Francis Herbert Wenham</a> in 1871) could model large-scale objects accurately. <a href="/wiki/BAE_Systems_Regional_Aircraft" title="BAE Systems Regional Aircraft">BAE Systems Regional Aircraft</a> assembled Britain\'s last airliner, the <a href="/wiki/British_Aerospace_146" title="British Aerospace 146">British Aerospace 146</a> (Avro RJX), at <a href="/wiki/Woodford,_Greater_Manchester" title="Woodford, Greater Manchester">Woodford</a> in November 2001. The Merlin-powered <a href="/wiki/Avro_Tudor" title="Avro Tudor">Avro Tudor</a> G-AGPF, which took off from what is now Manchester Airport on 14 June 1945, was Britain\'s first <a href="/wiki/Cabin_pressurization" title="Cabin pressurization">pressurised</a> civilian aircraft; only 38 were built and it was designed for the <a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_flight" title="Transatlantic flight">North Atlantic route</a>. On 13 May 1949, <i>VN799</i> the English Electric Canberra first flew from Warton: Warton at the time was a former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Air_Forces" title="United States Army Air Forces">USAAF</a> wartime maintenance base; the German <a href="/wiki/Arado_Ar_234" title="Arado Ar 234">Arado Ar 234</a> was technically the world\'s first jet bomber; the Canberra would be the first jet aircraft to make a non-stop crossing of the Atlantic on 21 February 1951.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Robert_Whitehead" title="Robert Whitehead">Robert Whitehead</a> of Bolton invented the modern-day <a href="/wiki/Whitehead_torpedo" title="Whitehead torpedo">torpedo</a> in 1866. Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Pickles_Hartley" title="William Pickles Hartley">William Pickles Hartley</a> of Lancashire founded <a href="/wiki/Hartley%27s" title="Hartley&#39;s">Hartley\'s</a> Jam in 1871, building a purpose-built village at <a href="/wiki/Aintree" title="Aintree">Aintree</a>. Sir <a href="/wiki/Henry_Tate" title="Henry Tate">Henry Tate</a> also came from Lancashire, joining <a href="/wiki/Abram_Lyle" title="Abram Lyle">Abram Lyle</a> in 1921, of whose <a href="/wiki/Golden_syrup" title="Golden syrup">Golden Syrup</a> tins are claimed to be Britain\'s oldest brand; he established the <a href="/wiki/Tate" title="Tate">Tate</a> Gallery in 1897. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hope-Jones" title="Robert Hope-Jones">Robert Hope-Jones</a> of the Wirral invented the <a href="/wiki/Wurlitzer_theatre_organs_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Wurlitzer theatre organs in the United Kingdom">Wurlitzer</a> organ. The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Christys%27_%26_Co&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Christys&#39; &amp; Co (page does not exist)">Christys\' &amp; Co</a> factory in Stockport was the largest hat-making factory in the world in the nineteenth century; it became part of <a href="/wiki/Associated_British_Hat_Manufacturers" title="Associated British Hat Manufacturers">Associated British Hat Manufacturers</a> and is now in <a href="/wiki/Oxfordshire" title="Oxfordshire">Oxfordshire</a>. The company owner\'s son founded <a href="/wiki/Christy_(towel_manufacturer)" title="Christy (towel manufacturer)">Christy</a> in 1850 in <a href="/wiki/Droylsden" title="Droylsden">Droylsden</a> (now in Tameside), which invented the industrially produced <a href="/wiki/Terrycloth" title="Terrycloth">towel</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Where_rabbits_once_hopped..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_119794.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Where_rabbits_once_hopped..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_119794.jpg/220px-Where_rabbits_once_hopped..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_119794.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Where_rabbits_once_hopped..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_119794.jpg/330px-Where_rabbits_once_hopped..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_119794.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Where_rabbits_once_hopped..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_119794.jpg/440px-Where_rabbits_once_hopped..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_119794.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="426" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Where_rabbits_once_hopped..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_119794.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>JLR at Halewood</div></div></div>\n<p>Britain\'s most popular car, the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Escort_(Europe)" title="Ford Escort (Europe)">Ford Escort</a>, was made throughout its life (until 21 July 2000) at Halewood by Ford; 5 million were made there from 1967. In 1998, production of its replacement the Focus was transferred to <a href="/wiki/Saarlouis_Body_%26_Assembly" title="Saarlouis Body &amp; Assembly">Saarlouis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ford_Valencia_Plant" title="Ford Valencia Plant">Valencia</a>, which signalled the end of the site\'s ownership by Ford. The <a href="/wiki/Jaguar_X-Type" title="Jaguar X-Type">Jaguar X-Type</a> was first made there in May 2001, until late 2009. In the UK, the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Mondeo" title="Ford Mondeo">Mondeo</a> has sold 1.4m since 1993, and is made in <a href="/wiki/Ford_Valencia_Plant" title="Ford Valencia Plant">Valencia</a> in Spain.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Starchaser_Industries" title="Starchaser Industries">Starchaser Industries</a> of Hyde is hoping to send a British citizen into space, on a <a href="/wiki/British_space_programme" title="British space programme">British rocket</a>; only Richard Branson will be able to compete for that honour; <a href="/wiki/British_Aircraft_Corporation" title="British Aircraft Corporation">BAC</a> at Preston had proposed its <a href="/wiki/MUSTARD" class="mw-redirect" title="MUSTARD">MUSTARD</a> re-usable spacecraft in 1964, which although not built had given <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> a concept.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_West_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Suffragette" title="Suffragette">Suffragette</a> movement came from Manchester\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Social_and_Political_Union" title="Women&#39;s Social and Political Union">Women\'s Social and Political Union</a> (WSPU). <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wynne" title="Arthur Wynne">Arthur Wynne</a>, born in Liverpool, invented the <a href="/wiki/Crossword" title="Crossword">crossword</a> in December 1913. On 13 August 1964, Britain carried out its last two executions at Strangeways and <a href="/wiki/HM_Prison_Liverpool" title="HM Prison Liverpool">Walton Prison</a>. Under the <a href="/wiki/Museums_Act_1845" title="Museums Act 1845">Museums Act 1845</a>, the UK\'s second and third <a href="/wiki/Public_library" title="Public library">public municipal librarys</a> were at Warrington in 1848 and at <a href="/wiki/Salford_Museum_and_Art_Gallery" title="Salford Museum and Art Gallery">Salford Museum and Art Gallery</a> in 1850; Canterbury had been first in 1847. The first <a href="/wiki/Trades_Union_Congress" title="Trades Union Congress">Trades Union Congress</a> was held in 1868 at the <a href="/wiki/Mechanics%27_Institute,_Manchester" title="Mechanics&#39; Institute, Manchester">Mechanics\' Institute, Manchester</a>. The <a href="/wiki/World_Pie_Eating_Championship" title="World Pie Eating Championship">World Pie Eating Championship</a> is held in Wigan each year.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ann_Lee" title="Ann Lee">Ann Lee</a> from Manchester started the USA <a href="/wiki/Shakers" title="Shakers">Shakers</a> movement, founded out of the <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakers</a>, which itself has strong links to <a href="/wiki/Pendle_Hill" title="Pendle Hill">Pendle Hill</a> in Lancashire. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Livesey" title="Joseph Livesey">Joseph Livesey</a> of Preston was the founder of Britain\'s <a href="/wiki/Temperance_movement" title="Temperance movement">temperance movement</a>, and the word <i>teetotal</i> was first coined in Preston in 1833. The crumbly <a href="/wiki/Cheshire_cheese" title="Cheshire cheese">Cheshire cheese</a> is thought to be the oldest in Britain. <a href="/wiki/Heaton_Park" title="Heaton Park">Heaton Park</a> in north Manchester is the largest municipal park in Europe. <a href="/wiki/Jelly_Babies" title="Jelly Babies">Jelly Babies</a> were invented in Lancaster in 1864, at Fryers of Lancashire. The first <a href="/wiki/KFC" title="KFC">KFC</a> outlet in the UK was on Fishergate in Preston in May 1965, opened by the entrepreneur Ray Allen. Oldham claims to be the site of the first <a href="/wiki/Fish_and_chips" title="Fish and chips">fried potatoes</a> in the UK in 1860. The UK\'s biggest dance music festival takes place on the August Bank Holiday at <a href="/wiki/Creamfields" title="Creamfields">Creamfields</a> on Daresbury Estate. <a href="/wiki/Ingvar_Kamprad" title="Ingvar Kamprad">Ingvar Kamprad</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/IKEA" title="IKEA">IKEA</a> opened its first UK store in Warrington on 1 October 1987; the UK was the 20th country at the time that IKEA had been established. The <a href="/wiki/International_Cheese_Awards" title="International Cheese Awards">International Cheese Awards</a> are held at the end of July in Nantwich.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>, the two largest cities in the North West by population, are known for being the birthplace of <a href="/wiki/Beat_music" title="Beat music">beat music</a> (also called "Merseybeat") during the 1960s to 1970s, and the development of the <a href="/wiki/Madchester" title="Madchester">Madchester</a> music scene from the 1980s, and 1990s respectively.\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/A_Taste_of_Honey_(film)" title="A Taste of Honey (film)">A Taste of Honey</a></i> was an influential 1960s film set in Salford, depicting <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working class</a> poverty in ways not previously seen at the cinema, known as <a href="/wiki/Kitchen_sink_realism" title="Kitchen sink realism">kitchen sink realism</a>; <a href="/wiki/Walter_Greenwood" title="Walter Greenwood">Walter Greenwood</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Love_on_the_Dole" title="Love on the Dole">Love on the Dole</a></i>, a 1930s book also set in Salford, was thought by the <a href="/wiki/British_Board_of_Film_Classification" title="British Board of Film Classification">BBFC</a> to be too sordid a depiction of poverty to be made into a film; <a href="/wiki/Mike_Leigh" title="Mike Leigh">Mike Leigh</a>, from Salford, has produced films on a similar subject.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_West_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queensway_Mersey_Tunnel_entrance_Liverpool_3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Queensway_Mersey_Tunnel_entrance_Liverpool_3.jpg/220px-Queensway_Mersey_Tunnel_entrance_Liverpool_3.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Queensway_Mersey_Tunnel_entrance_Liverpool_3.jpg/330px-Queensway_Mersey_Tunnel_entrance_Liverpool_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Queensway_Mersey_Tunnel_entrance_Liverpool_3.jpg/440px-Queensway_Mersey_Tunnel_entrance_Liverpool_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1984" data-file-height="1488" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queensway_Mersey_Tunnel_entrance_Liverpool_3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Queensway_Tunnel" title="Queensway Tunnel">Queensway Tunnel</a></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport_policy">Transport policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_West_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Transport policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As part of the national transport planning system,\nthe <a href="/wiki/North_West_Regional_Assembly" title="North West Regional Assembly">North West Regional Assembly</a> was (before its abolition in 2008) required to produce a Regional Transport Strategy (RTS) to provide long term planning for transport in the region. This involved region wide transport schemes such as those carried out by the <a href="/wiki/Highways_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="Highways Agency">Highways Agency</a> and <a href="/wiki/Network_Rail" title="Network Rail">Network Rail</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GoNW1_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GoNW1-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Within the region the local transport authorities plan for the future by producing <a href="/wiki/Local_transport_plan" title="Local transport plan">Local Transport Plans (LTP)</a> which outline their strategies, policies and implementation programmes.<sup id="cite_ref-DfT1_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DfT1-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The most recent LTP is that for the period 2006\xe2\x80\x9311. In the North West region, the following transport authorities have published their LTP online: <a href="/wiki/Blackburn_with_Darwen" title="Blackburn with Darwen">Blackburn with Darwen</a> U.A,<sup id="cite_ref-BwDLTP_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BwDLTP-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Blackpool" title="Blackpool">Blackpool</a> U.A.,<sup id="cite_ref-BpLTP_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BpLTP-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cheshire" title="Cheshire">Cheshire</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ChLTP_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChLTP-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greater_Manchester" title="Greater Manchester">Greater Manchester</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-GMLTP_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GMLTP-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Borough_of_Halton" title="Borough of Halton">Halton</a> U.A.,<sup id="cite_ref-LTP_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LTP-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-LaLTP_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LaLTP-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Merseyside" title="Merseyside">Merseyside</a><sup id="cite_ref-MsLTP_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MsLTP-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Warrington" title="Warrington">Warrington</a> U.A.<sup id="cite_ref-WrLTP_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WrLTP-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Since 1 April 2009, when the county of Cheshire was split into two unitary councils<sup id="cite_ref-ChGOV_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChGOV-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> the Cheshire transport authority ceased to exist, however it is the most recent LTP for the area.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Road">Road</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_West_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Road">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:M6_motorway_near_Carnforth.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/M6_motorway_near_Carnforth.jpg/200px-M6_motorway_near_Carnforth.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/M6_motorway_near_Carnforth.jpg/300px-M6_motorway_near_Carnforth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/M6_motorway_near_Carnforth.jpg/400px-M6_motorway_near_Carnforth.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:M6_motorway_near_Carnforth.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/M6_motorway" title="M6 motorway">M6 motorway</a> is one of the North West\'s principal roads</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Regionwide">Regionwide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_West_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Regionwide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hardknott_sign.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f6/Hardknott_sign.JPG/220px-Hardknott_sign.JPG" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f6/Hardknott_sign.JPG/330px-Hardknott_sign.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f6/Hardknott_sign.JPG/440px-Hardknott_sign.JPG 2x" data-file-width="533" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hardknott_sign.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Warning signs at Hardknott Pass</div></div></div>\n<p>Regionwide the principal road link is the <a href="/wiki/M6_motorway" title="M6 motorway">M6</a>, this enters the region (from <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>) near <a href="/wiki/Carlisle,_Cumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Carlisle, Cumbria">Carlisle</a> in the north and leaves it (for the <a href="/wiki/English_Midlands" class="mw-redirect" title="English Midlands">English Midlands</a>) near <a href="/wiki/Crewe" title="Crewe">Crewe</a> in the south. It connects such towns and cities as <a href="/wiki/Penrith,_Cumbria" title="Penrith, Cumbria">Penrith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kendal" title="Kendal">Kendal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lancaster,_Lancashire" title="Lancaster, Lancashire">Lancaster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Preston,_Lancashire" title="Preston, Lancashire">Preston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warrington" title="Warrington">Warrington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>. The M6 intersects many of the North West\'s motorways and <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_road_numbering_scheme" title="Great Britain road numbering scheme">A-roads</a>, and carries almost 120,000 vehicles per day (41,975,000 per year).<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Britain\'s most severe steep road is <a href="/wiki/Hardknott_Pass" title="Hardknott Pass">Hardknott Pass</a> in Cumbria, and the highest road in the UK is the former A6293 at 2,780&#160;ft at <a href="/wiki/Milburn,_Cumbria" title="Milburn, Cumbria">Milburn, Cumbria</a>; the highest classified road in England was the <a href="/wiki/A689_road" title="A689 road">A689</a> east of <a href="/wiki/Nenthead" title="Nenthead">Nenthead</a> in Cumbria on the Durham boundary.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stockport_viaduct_M60.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Stockport_viaduct_M60.jpg/200px-Stockport_viaduct_M60.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Stockport_viaduct_M60.jpg/300px-Stockport_viaduct_M60.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Stockport_viaduct_M60.jpg/400px-Stockport_viaduct_M60.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stockport_viaduct_M60.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Old meets new at the <a href="/wiki/Stockport_Viaduct" title="Stockport Viaduct">Stockport Viaduct</a>; designed by <a href="/wiki/George_W._Buck" title="George W. Buck">George W. Buck</a>, it is the largest brick structure in the UK, built in 1840, when it was the largest viaduct in the world; it features in many <a href="/wiki/L._S._Lowry" title="L. S. Lowry">L. S. Lowry</a> paintings</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greater_Manchester_and_Merseyside">Greater Manchester and Merseyside</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_West_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Greater Manchester and Merseyside">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Greater Manchester and Merseyside areas are home to almost 4 million people, and over half the region\'s population. The road networks intertwining these <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_area" title="Metropolitan area">metropolitan areas</a> are extremely important to the economy and are largely motorway, including the <a href="/wiki/M62_motorway" title="M62 motorway">M62</a> which crosses the entire country (east to west \xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Hull" title="Kingston upon Hull">Hull</a> to Liverpool), this motorway directly connects the cities of Manchester and Liverpool. The M62 sees 78,000 vehicles using the motorway in the North West per day.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The Merseyside-Manchester region has many motorways, that serve many millions on a daily basis, other include the <a href="/wiki/M61_motorway" title="M61 motorway">M61</a> which connects Manchester to Preston, the <a href="/wiki/M56_motorway" title="M56 motorway">M56</a> which runs south of Manchester to Cheshire and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, The <a href="/wiki/M57_motorway" title="M57 motorway">M57</a> and <a href="/wiki/M58_motorway" title="M58 motorway">M58 motorways</a> run north of Liverpool, and connect towns such as <a href="/wiki/St_Helens,_Merseyside" title="St Helens, Merseyside">St. Helens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wigan" title="Wigan">Wigan</a>. The <a href="/wiki/M60_motorway" title="M60 motorway">M60</a> is Manchester\'s <a href="/wiki/Ring_road" title="Ring road">ring road</a>, the <a href="/wiki/M67_motorway" title="M67 motorway">M67</a> and <a href="/wiki/M66_motorway" title="M66 motorway">M66 motorways</a> run east and north respectively, both of these motorways are under 10 miles (16&#160;km) and link Manchester to smaller outlying settlements. On top of this there are countless numbers of A-roads, B-roads and minor roads which circle, entwine and serve these two major metropolises. <i>For more information, see: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Manchester" title="Transport in Manchester">Transport in Manchester</a></i>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Border_Scotland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Border_Scotland.jpg/200px-Border_Scotland.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Border_Scotland.jpg/300px-Border_Scotland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Border_Scotland.jpg/400px-Border_Scotland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1350" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Border_Scotland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sign marking entry to Scotland located on the <a href="/wiki/M6_motorway" title="M6 motorway">M6 motorway</a> crossing the border.</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumbria">Cumbria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_West_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Cumbria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In Cumbria the M6 runs all the way down the east of the county connecting the very north of England to the Lancashire border. The <a href="/wiki/A590_road" title="A590 road">A590</a> links <a href="/wiki/Barrow-in-Furness" title="Barrow-in-Furness">Barrow-in-Furness</a> to <a href="/wiki/Kendal" title="Kendal">Kendal</a> with around 14,000 vehicles per day.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/A595_road" title="A595 road">A595</a> runs all the way along the West Cumbrian coast beginning near Barrow and ending in Carlisle, linking towns such as <a href="/wiki/Whitehaven" title="Whitehaven">Whitehaven</a> and <a href="/wiki/Workington" title="Workington">Workington</a>. The <a href="/wiki/A591_road" title="A591 road">A591 road</a> runs from Kendal to the centre of the county connecting <a href="/wiki/Lake_District" title="Lake District">Lake District</a> settlements like <a href="/wiki/Windermere,_Cumbria_(town)" title="Windermere, Cumbria (town)">Windermere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ambleside" title="Ambleside">Ambleside</a> and <a href="/wiki/Keswick,_Cumbria" title="Keswick, Cumbria">Keswick</a>. Other important A-roads include the A5092, <a href="/wiki/A66_road" title="A66 road">A66</a>, <a href="/wiki/A596_road" title="A596 road">A596</a> and formerly the <a href="/wiki/A74_road" title="A74 road">A74</a>, until this was upgraded to motorway standard as an extension of the M6 between 2006 and 2008 to meet the <a href="/wiki/A74(M)_and_M74_motorways" title="A74(M) and M74 motorways">A74(M)</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Scottish_border" title="Anglo-Scottish border">Scottish border</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lancashire">Lancashire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_West_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Lancashire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Lancashire economy relies strongly on the M6 which also runs from north to south (Lancaster to <a href="/wiki/Chorley" title="Chorley">Chorley</a>). Other motorways in the region include the fairly short <a href="/wiki/M55_motorway" title="M55 motorway">M55</a> which connects the city of Preston and the town of Blackpool at 11.5 miles (18.5&#160;km) in length. The <a href="/wiki/M65_motorway" title="M65 motorway">M65 motorway</a> runs from east to west starting in the town of <a href="/wiki/Colne" title="Colne">Colne</a>, running past <a href="/wiki/Burnley" title="Burnley">Burnley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Accrington" title="Accrington">Accrington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blackburn" title="Blackburn">Blackburn</a> and terminating in Preston. The Lancaster-<a href="/wiki/Morecambe" title="Morecambe">Morecambe</a> area is served by the <a href="/wiki/A683_road" class="mw-redirect" title="A683 road">A683</a>, A6 and A589 roads, the Blackpool-<a href="/wiki/The_Fylde" title="The Fylde">Fylde</a>-<a href="/wiki/Fleetwood" title="Fleetwood">Fleetwood</a> area is home to the <a href="/wiki/A587_road" title="A587 road">A587</a>, <a href="/wiki/A584_road" title="A584 road">A584</a>, <a href="/wiki/A583_road" title="A583 road">A583</a> and <a href="/wiki/A585_road" title="A585 road">A585 roads</a>. The city of Preston and its surroundings are served by the <a href="/wiki/A6_road_(Great_Britain)" class="mw-redirect" title="A6 road (Great Britain)">A6</a>, <a href="/wiki/A59_road_(Great_Britain)" class="mw-redirect" title="A59 road (Great Britain)">A59</a>, A585, A584, A583, A582 and to the very south-east, the <a href="/wiki/M61_motorway" title="M61 motorway">M61 motorway</a>. To the east of the county are the A59, A6119, A677, A679, A666, A680, A56, A646 and A682. The <a href="/wiki/M66_motorway" title="M66 motorway">M66</a> begins 500 metres (0.3&#160;mi) inside the county border near <a href="/wiki/Edenfield" title="Edenfield">Edenfield</a>, providing an important link between east Lancashire and Manchester.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cheshire">Cheshire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_West_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Cheshire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In Cheshire there are four motorways the M6, the M56 (linking Chester to the east), the M53 (linking Chester to <a href="/wiki/Birkenhead" title="Birkenhead">Birkenhead</a>) and the M62, which runs just along the county\'s northern border with Merseyside and Greater Manchester. The Cheshire road system is made up of 3,417 miles (5,499&#160;km) of highway, and the principal one (M6) carries 140,000<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> vehicles in the county daily, linking the North West to the <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_(region)" title="West Midlands (region)">West Midlands</a>.\n</p><p>The county town of Chester is served by the <a href="/wiki/A55_road" title="A55 road">A55</a>, <a href="/wiki/A483_road" title="A483 road">A483</a> and A494 roads amongst others. To the west of the M6, <a href="/wiki/Crewe" title="Crewe">Crewe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northwich" title="Northwich">Northwich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sandbach" title="Sandbach">Sandbach</a> are served by the <a href="/wiki/A54_road" title="A54 road">A54</a>, <a href="/wiki/A51_road" title="A51 road">A51</a>, <a href="/wiki/A49_road" title="A49 road">A49</a>, A533, A556 and A530 roads, these all eventually link up connecting the towns to the larger cities, including <a href="/wiki/Stoke-on-Trent" title="Stoke-on-Trent">Stoke-on-Trent</a> to the south. To the east of the M6 in Cheshire lies the <a href="/wiki/Peak_District" title="Peak District">Peak District</a>, and towns such as <a href="/wiki/Macclesfield" title="Macclesfield">Macclesfield</a> and <a href="/wiki/Congleton" title="Congleton">Congleton</a> which are served by the <a href="/wiki/A6_road_(Great_Britain)" class="mw-redirect" title="A6 road (Great Britain)">A6</a>, A537, A536, <a href="/wiki/A34_road_(Great_Britain)" class="mw-redirect" title="A34 road (Great Britain)">A34</a>, A523 and A566 roads.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air">Air</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_West_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Air">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The primary <a href="/wiki/International_airport" title="International airport">international airport</a> in the region is <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Airport" title="Manchester Airport">Manchester Airport</a>, which served 22.1 million passengers in 2007 (18.7 million of which were international),<sup id="cite_ref-caa_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-caa-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> more than some of the world\'s major aviation hubs. The airport is home to <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Airport#Terminals_and_destinations" title="Manchester Airport">three terminals</a> (plus the <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Airport#World_Freight_Terminal" title="Manchester Airport">World Freight Terminal</a>), which serve destinations worldwide. The largest airlines at the airport in terms of flights in 2007 were <a href="/wiki/Flybe" title="Flybe">Flybe</a>, <a href="/wiki/BMI_(airline)" class="mw-redirect" title="BMI (airline)">BMI</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Airways" title="British Airways">British Airways</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jet2.com" title="Jet2.com">Jet2.com</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lufthansa" title="Lufthansa">Lufthansa</a>, although several long-haul carriers such as <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines" title="American Airlines">American Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delta_Air_Lines" title="Delta Air Lines">Delta Air Lines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Atlantic_Airways" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Atlantic Airways">Virgin Atlantic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore_Airlines" title="Singapore Airlines">Singapore Airlines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emirates_(airline)" title="Emirates (airline)">Emirates</a> also operate from the airport.\n</p><p>In 2012 Manchester Airport was the third-busiest airport in the UK with 19m passengers, after Gatwick then Heathrow. <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Airports_Group" title="Manchester Airports Group">Manchester Airports Group</a> is owned approximately one-third by Manchester Council, and one-third by the other nine Greater Manchester councils.\n</p><p>In 2007 Manchester had a recorded 222,703 aircraft movements,<sup id="cite_ref-caa_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-caa-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> the airport is also a <a href="/wiki/Airline_hub" title="Airline hub">hub</a> for major holiday airlines such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cook_Airlines" title="Thomas Cook Airlines">Thomas Cook Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monarch_Airlines" title="Monarch Airlines">Monarch Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_Choice_Airways" title="First Choice Airways">First Choice Airways</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomson_Airways" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomson Airways">Thomson Airways</a>.\n</p><p>The region\'s second largest, but fastest growing airport is <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_John_Lennon_Airport" title="Liverpool John Lennon Airport">Liverpool John Lennon Airport</a>, where passenger numbers have increased from around 690,000 in 1997 to nearly 5.5 million in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-caa_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-caa-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The airport serves destinations primarily in the UK and Europe and is a major hub for <a href="/wiki/EasyJet" title="EasyJet">EasyJet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ryanair" title="Ryanair">Ryanair</a>.\n</p><p>The only other significant passenger airport in the region was <a href="/wiki/Blackpool_International_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Blackpool International Airport">Blackpool Airport</a>, which was refurbished in 2006 and handled around half a million passengers annually. Destinations ranged from the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> in Spain to the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a>, but commercial flights ended in March 2017.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Manchester_Airport.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Manchester_Airport.jpg/220px-Manchester_Airport.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Manchester_Airport.jpg/330px-Manchester_Airport.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Manchester_Airport.jpg/440px-Manchester_Airport.jpg 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="320" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Manchester_Airport.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Manchester Airport aerial view</div></div></div>\n<p><b>Cumbria</b>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Barrow/Walney_Island_Airfield" class="mw-redirect" title="Barrow/Walney Island Airfield">Barrow/Walney Island Airfield</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Operated by <a href="/wiki/BAE_Systems_Submarine_Solutions" class="mw-redirect" title="BAE Systems Submarine Solutions">BAE Systems Marine Ltd \xe2\x80\x93 Submarines</a>, private use</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Carlisle_Lake_District_Airport" title="Carlisle Lake District Airport">Carlisle Lake District Airport</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Operated by <a href="/wiki/Stobart_Group" title="Stobart Group">Stobart Air Ltd</a>, public use</li></ul>\n<p><b>Greater Manchester</b>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/City_Airport_Manchester" class="mw-redirect" title="City Airport Manchester">City Airport Manchester</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Operated by City Airport Manchester Ltd, public use</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Manchester_Airport" title="Manchester Airport">Manchester Airport</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Major international airport operated by <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Airport_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Manchester Airport Group">Manchester Airport Group</a>, destinations worldwide</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Woodford_Aerodrome" title="Woodford Aerodrome">Woodford Aerodrome</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Operated by <a href="/wiki/BAE_Systems_Regional_Aircraft" title="BAE Systems Regional Aircraft">BAE Systems Regional Aircraft</a>, now closed</li></ul>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Woodvale08_024.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Woodvale08_024.JPG/220px-Woodvale08_024.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Woodvale08_024.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Grob_G_115" title="Grob G 115">Tutor</a> aircraft at RAF Woodvale</div></div></div>\n<p><b>Lancashire</b>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Blackpool_International_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Blackpool International Airport">Blackpool International Airport</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Operated by <a href="/wiki/Balfour_Beatty" title="Balfour Beatty">Balfour Beatty</a>, public use (commercial flights withdrawn)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Warton_Aerodrome" title="Warton Aerodrome">Warton Aerodrome</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Operated by <a href="/wiki/BAE_Systems" title="BAE Systems">BAE Systems</a>, private use</li></ul>\n<p><b>Merseyside</b>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Liverpool_John_Lennon_Airport" title="Liverpool John Lennon Airport">Liverpool John Lennon Airport</a> \xe2\x80\x93 International airport operated by Liverpool Airport plc, destinations worldwide</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/RAF_Woodvale" title="RAF Woodvale">RAF Woodvale</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Operated by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a>, military use</li>\n<li>Southport Birkdale Sands airstrip \xe2\x80\x93 Sand runway located on Southport beach (infrequent use, subject to prior permission)</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rail">Rail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_West_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Rail">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Piccadilly_Station_Manchester_-_geograph.org.uk_-_692981.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Piccadilly_Station_Manchester_-_geograph.org.uk_-_692981.jpg/200px-Piccadilly_Station_Manchester_-_geograph.org.uk_-_692981.jpg" width="200" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Piccadilly_Station_Manchester_-_geograph.org.uk_-_692981.jpg/300px-Piccadilly_Station_Manchester_-_geograph.org.uk_-_692981.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Piccadilly_Station_Manchester_-_geograph.org.uk_-_692981.jpg/400px-Piccadilly_Station_Manchester_-_geograph.org.uk_-_692981.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="534" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Piccadilly_Station_Manchester_-_geograph.org.uk_-_692981.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Manchester\'s <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Piccadilly_station" title="Manchester Piccadilly station">Piccadilly station</a> is the largest and busiest railway station in the region.</div></div></div>\n<p>The main connection by train is the <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_Main_Line" title="West Coast Main Line">West Coast Main Line</a> (<a href="/wiki/Virgin_Trains" title="Virgin Trains">Virgin Trains</a>), connecting most of the North West. Other important lines are the <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_to_Manchester_Lines" class="mw-redirect" title="Liverpool to Manchester Lines">Liverpool to Manchester Lines</a> and the <a href="/wiki/North_TransPennine" class="mw-redirect" title="North TransPennine">North TransPennine</a> which connects <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_Lime_Street_railway_station" title="Liverpool Lime Street railway station">Liverpool</a> to <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Piccadilly_station" title="Manchester Piccadilly station">Manchester</a> through <a href="/wiki/Warrington_Central_railway_station" title="Warrington Central railway station">Warrington</a>. East-west connections in Lancashire are carried via the <a href="/wiki/Caldervale_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Caldervale Line">Caldervale Line</a> to <a href="/wiki/Blackpool_North_railway_station" title="Blackpool North railway station">Blackpool</a>. Liverpool and Manchester both have extensive local passenger rail networks operating high-frequency commuter trains. The quietest train station in the region is <a href="/wiki/Reddish_South_railway_station" title="Reddish South railway station">Reddish South</a>, the 4th quietest in Britain.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/InterCity_(British_Rail)" title="InterCity (British Rail)">InterCity</a> service in the UK began between London and Manchester in the late 1960s; the new <a href="/wiki/Euston_railway_station" title="Euston railway station">Euston station</a> opened in 1968. With the new electrification of the line in the late 1960s, passenger numbers doubled.\n</p><p>The region saw the last steam-train service on the UK network - the <a href="/wiki/Fifteen_Guinea_Special" title="Fifteen Guinea Special">Fifteen Guinea Special</a> on 11 August 1968, with\nthree <a href="/wiki/LMS_Stanier_Class_5_4-6-0" title="LMS Stanier Class 5 4-6-0">Black Five</a> locomotives.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea">Sea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_West_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Sea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Sea ferries depart from <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Liverpool" title="Port of Liverpool">Liverpool</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gladstone_Dock" title="Gladstone Dock">Gladstone Dock</a>) to <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> (<a href="/wiki/P%26O_Irish_Sea" title="P&amp;O Irish Sea">P&amp;O Irish Sea</a>) and to <a href="/wiki/Douglas,_Isle_of_Man" title="Douglas, Isle of Man">Douglas</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> (<a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man_Steam_Packet" class="mw-redirect" title="Isle of Man Steam Packet">Isle of Man Steam Packet</a>); <a href="/wiki/Birkenhead" title="Birkenhead">Birkenhead</a> (<a href="/wiki/Twelve_Quays" title="Twelve Quays">Twelve Quays Terminal</a>) to <a href="/wiki/Belfast" title="Belfast">Belfast</a> and Dublin (<a href="/wiki/Norfolkline" title="Norfolkline">Norfolkline Irish Sea Ferries</a> \xe2\x80\x93 former <a href="/wiki/Norse_Merchant_Ferries" title="Norse Merchant Ferries">Norse Merchant Ferries</a>); <a href="/wiki/Fleetwood" title="Fleetwood">Fleetwood</a> to <a href="/wiki/Larne" title="Larne">Larne</a> (<a href="/wiki/Stena_Line" title="Stena Line">Stena Line</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Heysham" title="Heysham">Heysham</a> to Douglas (Isle of Man Steam Packet).\n</p><p>The world\'s first hovercraft service took place on 20 July 1962 from <a href="/wiki/Leasowe" title="Leasowe">Leasowe</a> (<a href="/wiki/Moreton,_Merseyside" title="Moreton, Merseyside">Moreton</a>) to Rhyl, operated by <a href="/wiki/British_United_Airways" title="British United Airways">British United Airways</a>, in a <a href="/wiki/Vickers-Armstrongs" title="Vickers-Armstrongs">Vickers-Armstrongs</a> VA-3, powered by two <a href="/wiki/Turboprop" title="Turboprop">turboprop</a> engines.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Regional_Profile_of_the_North_West.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Regional_Profile_of_the_North_West.png/220px-Regional_Profile_of_the_North_West.png" width="220" height="457" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Regional_Profile_of_the_North_West.png/330px-Regional_Profile_of_the_North_West.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Regional_Profile_of_the_North_West.png/440px-Regional_Profile_of_the_North_West.png 2x" data-file-width="2917" data-file-height="6054" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Regional_Profile_of_the_North_West.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Regional profile of the North West</div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_West_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The North West is historically linked with the textiles industry, mainly before the mid 20th century. The Greater Manchester region produces <a href="/wiki/List_of_ceremonial_counties_in_England_by_gross_value_added" title="List of ceremonial counties in England by gross value added">the most economic output according to GVA in 2007</a> with \xc2\xa346,189m. Followed by Lancashire with \xc2\xa322,470m, Cheshire \xc2\xa321,317m and Merseyside \xc2\xa319,112m and Cumbria with \xc2\xa37,379m.\n</p><p>According to research by <a href="/wiki/Cushman_and_Wakefield" class="mw-redirect" title="Cushman and Wakefield">Cushman and Wakefield</a> in 2008, Manchester is the second best city to locate a business in the UK whilst Liverpool is the eleventh best city.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">Financial Times</a></i> stated that the North West economy, led by the redevelopment of Manchester and Liverpool, is a genuine rival to \'overheated London\'.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The area\'s electricity, formerly looked after by <a href="/wiki/MANWEB" title="MANWEB">MANWEB</a> and <a href="/wiki/NORWEB" title="NORWEB">NORWEB</a>, is now looked after by <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Power" title="Scottish Power">ScottishPower Energy Networks</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Utilities" title="United Utilities">United Utilities</a> respectively. The <a href="/wiki/Rampside_Gas_Terminal" title="Rampside Gas Terminal">Morecambe Bay gas field</a> provides 6% of the UK\'s natural gas.\n</p>\n<div role="navigation" class="navbox" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stanlow_from_a_distance_-_geograph.org.uk_-_684459.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Essar_Energy" title="Essar Energy">Essar Energy</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Stanlow_Refinery" title="Stanlow Refinery">Stanlow Refinery</a>, the UK\'s second largest refinery after <a href="/wiki/Fawley_Refinery" title="Fawley Refinery">Fawley</a>, looking north-east from <a href="/wiki/Wervin" title="Wervin">Wervin</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vauxhall%27s,_Ellesmere_Port_-_geograph.org.uk_-_218503.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Vauxhall%27s%2C_Ellesmere_Port_-_geograph.org.uk_-_218503.jpg/220px-Vauxhall%27s%2C_Ellesmere_Port_-_geograph.org.uk_-_218503.jpg" width="220"
